Raw diamond crystal.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png/220px-WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png/330px-WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png/440px-WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png 2x" data-file-width="1687" data-file-height="1133" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WOA05_GLODAP_pd_DIC_AYool.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Present day" (1990s) sea surface <a href="/wiki/Total_inorganic_carbon" title="Total inorganic carbon">dissolved inorganic carbon</a> concentration (from the <a href="/wiki/Global_Ocean_Data_Analysis_Project" title="Global Ocean Data Analysis Project">GLODAP</a> <a href="/wiki/Climatology" title="Climatology">climatology</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon is the <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">fourth most abundant chemical element</a> in the universe by mass after hydrogen, helium, and oxygen. Carbon is abundant in the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, and in the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_body%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Celestial body's atmosphere">atmospheres</a> of most <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-47">[47]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> contain microscopic diamonds that were formed when the <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a> was still a <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disk</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Microscopic diamonds may also be formed by the intense pressure and high temperature at the sites of meteorite impacts.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014 <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> announced a <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.astrochem.org/pahdb/">greatly upgraded database</a> for tracking <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> (PAHs) in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>. More than 20% of the carbon in the universe may be associated with PAHs, complex compounds of carbon and hydrogen without oxygen.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> These compounds figure in the <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">PAH world hypothesis</a> where they are hypothesized to have a role in <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a> and formation of <a href="/wiki/Life#Extraterrestrial_life" title="Life">life</a>. PAHs seem to have been formed "a couple of billion years" after the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>, are widespread throughout the universe, and are associated with <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">new stars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Exoplanets" class="mw-redirect" title="Exoplanets">exoplanets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been estimated that the solid earth as a whole contains 730 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> of carbon, with 2000 ppm in the core and 120 ppm in the combined mantle and crust.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Since the mass of the earth is <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7024597200000000000♠</span>5.972<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">×</span>10<sup>24</sup>&#160;kg</span>, this would imply 4360 million <a href="/wiki/Gigatonne" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigatonne">gigatonnes</a> of carbon. This is much more than the amount of carbon in the oceans or atmosphere (below).</p>
<p>In combination with <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, carbon is found in the Earth's atmosphere (approximately 810&#160;gigatonnes of carbon) and dissolved in all water bodies (approximately 36,000&#160;gigatonnes of carbon). Around 1,900&#160;gigatonnes of carbon are present in the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocarbons">Hydrocarbons</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>) contain carbon as well. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">"reserves" (not "resources")</a> amount to around 900&#160;gigatonnes with perhaps 18,000 Gt of resources.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">Oil reserves</a> are around 150&#160;gigatonnes. Proven sources of natural gas are about 175&#160;10<sup>12</sup> cubic metres (containing about 105 gigatonnes of carbon), but studies estimate another 900&#160;10<sup>12</sup> cubic metres of "unconventional" deposits such as <a href="/wiki/Shale_gas" title="Shale gas">shale gas</a>, representing about 540 gigatonnes of carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon is also found in <a href="/wiki/Methane_hydrates" class="mw-redirect" title="Methane hydrates">methane hydrates</a> in polar regions and under the seas. Various estimates put this carbon between 500, 2500 <a href="/wiki/Gigatonne" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigatonne">Gt</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> or 3,000 Gt.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the past, quantities of hydrocarbons were greater. According to one source, in the period from 1751 to 2008 about 347 gigatonnes of carbon were released as carbon dioxide to the atmosphere from burning of fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Another source puts the amount added to the atmosphere for the period since 1750 at 879 Gt, and the total going to the atmosphere, sea, and land (such as <a href="/wiki/Peat_bogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Peat bogs">peat bogs</a>) at almost 2,000 Gt.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon is a constituent (about 12% by mass) of the very large masses of <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> rock (<a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolomite" title="Dolomite">dolomite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marble" title="Marble">marble</a> and so on). <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> is very rich in carbon (<a href="/wiki/Anthracite" title="Anthracite">anthracite</a> contains 92–98%)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and is the largest commercial source of mineral carbon, accounting for 4,000&#160;gigatonnes or 80% of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>As for individual carbon allotropes, graphite is found in large quantities in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> (mostly in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" class="mw-redirect" title="New York (state)">New York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>), <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. Natural diamonds occur in the rock <a href="/wiki/Kimberlite" title="Kimberlite">kimberlite</a>, found in ancient <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> "necks", or "pipes". Most diamond deposits are in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, notably in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">Republic of the Congo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>. Diamond deposits have also been found in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, the Russian <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, and in Northern and Western <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>. Diamonds are now also being recovered from the ocean floor off the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>. Diamonds are found naturally, but about 30% of all industrial diamonds used in the U.S. are now manufactured.</p>
<p>Carbon-14 is formed in upper layers of the troposphere and the stratosphere at altitudes of 9–15&#160;km by a reaction that is precipitated by <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thermal_neutron" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal neutron">Thermal neutrons</a> are produced that collide with the nuclei of nitrogen-14, forming carbon-14 and a proton. As such, 1.2 × 10<sup>10</sup>% of atmospheric carbon dioxide contains carbon-14.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood276_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood276-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon-rich asteroids are relatively preponderant in the outer parts of the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> in our <a href="/wiki/Solar_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar system">solar system</a>. These asteroids have not yet been directly sampled by scientists. The asteroids can be used in hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">space-based carbon mining</a>, which may be possible in the future, but is currently technologically impossible.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_carbon" title="Isotopes of carbon">Isotopes of carbon</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">Isotopes</a> of carbon are <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nuclei</a> that contain six <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> plus a number of <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> (varying from 2 to 16). Carbon has two stable, naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-isotopes_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-15">[15]</a></sup> The isotope <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> (<sup>12</sup>C) forms 98.93% of the carbon on Earth, while <a href="/wiki/Carbon-13" title="Carbon-13">carbon-13</a> (<sup>13</sup>C) forms the remaining 1.07%.<sup id="cite_ref-isotopes_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-isotopes-15">[15]</a></sup> The concentration of <sup>12</sup>C is further increased in biological materials because biochemical reactions discriminate against <sup>13</sup>C.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In 1961, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry</a> (IUPAC) adopted the isotope <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">carbon-12</a> as the basis for <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weights</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Identification of carbon in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">nuclear magnetic resonance</a> (NMR) experiments is done with the isotope <sup>13</sup>C.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon-14" title="Carbon-14">Carbon-14</a> (<sup>14</sup>C) is a naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioisotope">radioisotope</a>, created in the <a href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Upper atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> (lower <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a> and upper <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>) by interaction of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> with <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> It is found in trace amounts on Earth of up to 1 part per <a href="/wiki/10%5E12" class="mw-redirect" title="10^12">trillion</a> (0.0000000001%), mostly confined to the atmosphere and superficial deposits, particularly of <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> and other organic materials.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> This isotope decays by 0.158 MeV <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>−</sup> emission</a>. Because of its relatively short <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 5730&#160;years, <sup>14</sup>C is virtually absent in ancient rocks. The amount of <sup>14</sup>C in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and in living organisms is almost constant, but decreases predictably in their bodies after death. This principle is used in <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">radiocarbon dating</a>, invented in 1949, which has been used extensively to determine the age of carbonaceous materials with ages up to about 40,000&#160;years.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are 15 known isotopes of carbon and the shortest-lived of these is <sup>8</sup>C which decays through <a href="/wiki/Proton_emission" title="Proton emission">proton emission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alpha_decay" title="Alpha decay">alpha decay</a> and has a half-life of 1.98739x10<sup>−21</sup> <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The exotic <sup>19</sup>C exhibits a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_halo" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear halo">nuclear halo</a>, which means its <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">radius</a> is appreciably larger than would be expected if the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> were a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a> of constant <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_in_stars">Formation in stars</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">Triple-alpha process</a> and <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a></div>
<p>Formation of the carbon atomic nucleus requires a nearly simultaneous triple collision of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_particle" title="Alpha particle">alpha particles</a> (<a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> nuclei) within the core of a <a href="/wiki/Giant_star" title="Giant star">giant</a> or <a href="/wiki/Supergiant" class="mw-redirect" title="Supergiant">supergiant</a> star which is known as the <a href="/wiki/Triple-alpha_process" title="Triple-alpha process">triple-alpha process</a>, as the products of further nuclear fusion reactions of helium with hydrogen or another helium nucleus produce <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lithium" title="Isotopes of lithium">lithium-5</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_beryllium" title="Isotopes of beryllium">beryllium-8</a> respectively, both of which are highly unstable and decay almost instantly back into smaller nuclei.<sup id="cite_ref-Audi_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Audi-69">[69]</a></sup> This happens in conditions of temperatures over 100 megakelvin and helium concentration that the rapid expansion and cooling of the early universe prohibited, and therefore no significant carbon was created during the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a>.</p>
<p>According to current physical cosmology theory, carbon is formed in the interiors of stars in the horizontal branch by the collision and transformation of three helium nuclei.<sup id="cite_ref-Ostlie_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ostlie-70">[70]</a></sup> When those stars die as supernova, the carbon is scattered into space as dust. This dust becomes component material for the formation of second or <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">third-generation star</a> systems with accreted planets.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20140221_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140221-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> is one such star system with an abundance of carbon, enabling the existence of life as we know it.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/CNO_cycle" title="CNO cycle">CNO cycle</a> is an additional fusion mechanisms that powers stars, wherein carbon operates as a <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>.</p>
<p>Rotational transitions of various isotopic forms of carbon monoxide (for example, <sup>12</sup>CO, <sup>13</sup>CO, and <sup>18</sup>CO) are detectable in the <a href="/wiki/Submillimetre_astronomy" title="Submillimetre astronomy">submillimeter</a> wavelength range, and are used in the study of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">newly forming stars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clouds" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular clouds">molecular clouds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_cycle">Carbon cycle</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg/300px-Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg.png" width="300" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg/450px-Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg/600px-Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="417" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_cycle-cute_diagram.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diagram of the carbon cycle. The black numbers indicate how much carbon is stored in various reservoirs, in billions tonnes ("GtC" stands for gigatonnes of carbon; figures are circa 2004). The purple numbers indicate how much carbon moves between reservoirs each year. The sediments, as defined in this diagram, do not include the ≈70&#160;million GtC of carbonate rock and <a href="/wiki/Kerogen" title="Kerogen">kerogen</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under terrestrial conditions, conversion of one element to another is very rare. Therefore, the amount of carbon on Earth is effectively constant. Thus, processes that use carbon must obtain it from somewhere and dispose of it somewhere else. The paths of carbon in the environment form the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> plants draw <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> from the atmosphere (or seawater) and build it into biomass, as in the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a>, a process of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>. Some of this biomass is eaten by animals, while some carbon is exhaled by animals as carbon dioxide. The carbon cycle is considerably more complicated than this short loop; for example, some carbon dioxide is dissolved in the oceans; if bacteria do not consume it, dead plant or animal matter may become <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> or <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, which releases carbon when burned.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compounds_of_carbon" title="Compounds of carbon">Compounds of carbon</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_compounds">Organic compounds</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Methane-2D-stereo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/150px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png" width="150" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/225px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Methane-2D-stereo.svg/300px-Methane-2D-stereo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="528" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Methane-2D-stereo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Structural formula of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, the simplest possible organic compound.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/300px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" width="300" height="349" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/450px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Auto-and_heterotrophs.png/600px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.png 2x" data-file-width="2722" data-file-height="3167" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Correlation between the <i>carbon cycle</i> and formation of organic compounds. In plants, carbon dioxide formed by carbon fixation can join with water in <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> (<span style="color:green;">green</span>) to form organic compounds, which can be used and further converted by both plants and animals.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon can form very long chains of interconnecting <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond" title="Carbon–carbon bond">carbon–carbon bonds</a>, a property that is called <a href="/wiki/Catenation" title="Catenation">catenation</a>. Carbon-carbon bonds are strong and stable. Through catenation, carbon forms a countless number of compounds. A tally of unique compounds shows that more contain carbon that those that do not.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A similar claim can be made for hydrogen because most organic compounds also contain hydrogen.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The simplest form of an organic molecule is the <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>—a large family of <a href="/wiki/Organic_molecule" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic molecule">organic molecules</a> that are composed of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> atoms bonded to a chain of carbon atoms. Chain length, side chains and <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional groups</a> all affect the properties of organic molecules.</p>
<p>Carbon occurs in all known <a href="/wiki/Organic_material" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic material">organic</a> life and is the basis of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemistry</a>. When united with <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>, it forms various <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> that are important to industry as <a href="/wiki/Refrigerants" class="mw-redirect" title="Refrigerants">refrigerants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lubricants" class="mw-redirect" title="Lubricants">lubricants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solvents" class="mw-redirect" title="Solvents">solvents</a>, as chemical feedstock for the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Petrochemicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Petrochemicals">petrochemicals</a>, and as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.</p>
<p>When combined with oxygen and hydrogen, carbon can form many groups of important biological compounds including <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lignan" title="Lignan">lignans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chitin" title="Chitin">chitins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>, and aromatic <a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carotenoids" class="mw-redirect" title="Carotenoids">carotenoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terpenes" class="mw-redirect" title="Terpenes">terpenes</a>. With <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> it forms <a href="/wiki/Alkaloid" title="Alkaloid">alkaloids</a>, and with the addition of sulfur also it forms <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rubber" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubber">rubber</a> products. With the addition of phosphorus to these other elements, it forms <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>, the chemical-code carriers of life, and <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the most important energy-transfer molecule in all living cells.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inorganic_compounds">Inorganic compounds</span></h3>
<p>Commonly carbon-containing compounds which are associated with minerals or which do not contain hydrogen or fluorine, are treated separately from classical <a href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic compounds">organic compounds</a>; the definition is not rigid (see reference articles above). Among these are the simple oxides of carbon. The most prominent oxide is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>). This was once the principal constituent of the <a href="/wiki/Paleoatmosphere" title="Paleoatmosphere">paleoatmosphere</a>, but is a minor component of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> today.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Dissolved in <a href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" class="mw-redirect" title="Water (molecule)">water</a>, it forms <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span>CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span></span>), but as most compounds with multiple single-bonded oxygens on a single carbon it is unstable.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Through this intermediate, though, resonance-stabilized <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">carbonate</a> <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> are produced. Some important minerals are carbonates, notably <a href="/wiki/Calcite" title="Calcite">calcite</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">Carbon disulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CS<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>) is similar.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood289_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood289-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>The other common oxide is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO). It is formed by incomplete combustion, and is a colorless, odorless gas. The molecules each contain a triple bond and are fairly <a href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" class="mw-redirect" title="Polar molecule">polar</a>, resulting in a tendency to bind permanently to hemoglobin molecules, displacing oxygen, which has a lower binding affinity.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">Cyanide</a> (CN<sup>−</sup>), has a similar structure, but behaves much like a <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halide</a> ion (<a href="/wiki/Pseudohalogen" title="Pseudohalogen">pseudohalogen</a>). For example, it can form the nitride <a href="/wiki/Cyanogen" title="Cyanogen">cyanogen</a> molecule ((CN)<sub>2</sub>), similar to diatomic halides. Other uncommon oxides are <a href="/wiki/Carbon_suboxide" title="Carbon suboxide">carbon suboxide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
3</span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> the unstable <a href="/wiki/Dicarbon_monoxide" title="Dicarbon monoxide">dicarbon monoxide</a> (C<sub>2</sub>O),<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_trioxide" title="Carbon trioxide">carbon trioxide</a> (CO<sub>3</sub>),<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclopentanepentone" title="Cyclopentanepentone">cyclopentanepentone</a> (C<sub>5</sub>O<sub>5</sub>),<sup id="cite_ref-fatiadi_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fatiadi-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cyclohexanehexone" title="Cyclohexanehexone">cyclohexanehexone</a> (C<sub>6</sub>O<sub>6</sub>),<sup id="cite_ref-fatiadi_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fatiadi-84">[84]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mellitic_anhydride" title="Mellitic anhydride">mellitic anhydride</a> (C<sub>12</sub>O<sub>9</sub>).</p>
<p>With reactive <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Tungsten" title="Tungsten">tungsten</a>, carbon forms either <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">carbides</a> (C<sup>4−</sup>) or <a href="/wiki/Acetylide" title="Acetylide">acetylides</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">C<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">2−<br />
2</span></span>) to form alloys with high melting points. These anions are also associated with <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, both very weak <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>. With an electronegativity of 2.5,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> carbon prefers to form <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>. A few carbides are covalent lattices, like <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">carborundum</a> (SiC), which resembles <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a>. Nevertheless, even the most polar and salt-like of carbides are not completely ionic compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood297_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood297-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organometallic_compounds">Organometallic compounds</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">Organometallic chemistry</a></div>
<p>Organometallic compounds by definition contain at least one carbon-metal bond. A wide range of such compounds exist; major classes include simple alkyl-metal compounds (for example, <a href="/wiki/Tetraethyllead" title="Tetraethyllead">tetraethyllead</a>), η<sup>2</sup>-alkene compounds (for example, <a href="/wiki/Zeise%27s_salt" title="Zeise's salt">Zeise's salt</a>), and η<sup>3</sup>-allyl compounds (for example, <a href="/wiki/Allylpalladium_chloride_dimer" title="Allylpalladium chloride dimer">allylpalladium chloride dimer</a>); <a href="/wiki/Metallocene" title="Metallocene">metallocenes</a> containing cyclopentadienyl ligands (for example, <a href="/wiki/Ferrocene" title="Ferrocene">ferrocene</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal_carbene_complex" title="Transition metal carbene complex">transition metal carbene complexes</a>. Many <a href="/wiki/Metal_carbonyl" title="Metal carbonyl">metal carbonyls</a> exist (for example, <a href="/wiki/Tetracarbonylnickel" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetracarbonylnickel">tetracarbonylnickel</a>); some workers consider the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> ligand to be purely inorganic, and not organometallic.</p>
<p>While carbon is understood to exclusively form four bonds, an interesting compound containing an octahedral hexacoordinated carbon atom has been reported. The cation of the compound is [(Ph<sub>3</sub>PAu)<sub>6</sub>C]<sup>2+</sup>. This phenomenon has been attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Aurophilicity" title="Aurophilicity">aurophilicity</a> of the gold ligands.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, it was confirmed that <a href="/wiki/Hexamethylbenzene" title="Hexamethylbenzene">hexamethylbenzene</a> contains a carbon atom with six bonds, rather than the usual four.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_etymology">History and etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg/170px-Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" width="170" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg/255px-Antoine_lavoisier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/96/Antoine_lavoisier.jpg 2x" data-file-width="303" data-file-height="318" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Antoine_lavoisier.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> in his youth</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> name <i>carbon</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>carbo</i> for coal and charcoal,<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> whence also comes the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> <i>charbon</i>, meaning charcoal. In <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>, the names for carbon are <i>Kohlenstoff</i>, <i>koolstof</i> and <i>kulstof</i> respectively, all literally meaning <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>-substance.</p>
<p>Carbon was discovered in prehistory and was known in the forms of <a href="/wiki/Soot" title="Soot">soot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> to the earliest <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a>. Diamonds were known probably as early as 2500&#160;BCE in China, while carbon in the form of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> was made around Roman times by the same chemistry as it is today, by heating wood in a <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramid</a> covered with <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> to exclude air.<sup id="cite_ref-ancient_China_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ancient_China-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png/170px-Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png" width="170" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png/255px-Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png/340px-Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="692" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele_from_Familj-Journalen1874.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1722, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Antoine_Ferchault_de_R%C3%A9aumur" title="René Antoine Ferchault de Réaumur">René Antoine Ferchault de Réaumur</a> demonstrated that iron was transformed into steel through the absorption of some substance, now known to be carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> In 1772, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Antoine Lavoisier</a> showed that diamonds are a form of carbon; when he burned samples of charcoal and diamond and found that neither produced any water and that both released the same amount of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> per <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">gram</a>. In 1779,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Wilhelm_Scheele" title="Carl Wilhelm Scheele">Carl Wilhelm Scheele</a> showed that graphite, which had been thought of as a form of <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, was instead identical with charcoal but with a small admixture of iron, and that it gave "aerial acid" (his name for carbon dioxide) when oxidized with nitric acid.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 1786, the French scientists <a href="/wiki/Claude_Louis_Berthollet" title="Claude Louis Berthollet">Claude Louis Berthollet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gaspard_Monge" title="Gaspard Monge">Gaspard Monge</a> and C. A. Vandermonde confirmed that graphite was mostly carbon by oxidizing it in oxygen in much the same way Lavoisier had done with diamond.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Some iron again was left, which the French scientists thought was necessary to the graphite structure. In their publication they proposed the name <i>carbone</i> (Latin <i>carbonum</i>) for the element in graphite which was given off as a gas upon burning graphite. Antoine Lavoisier then listed carbon as an <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">element</a> in his 1789 textbook.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>A new <a href="/wiki/Allotrope" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotrope">allotrope</a> of carbon, <a href="/wiki/Fullerene" title="Fullerene">fullerene</a>, that was discovered in 1985<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> includes <a href="/wiki/Nanostructure" title="Nanostructure">nanostructured</a> forms such as <a href="/wiki/Buckyball_(molecule)" class="mw-redirect" title="Buckyball (molecule)">buckyballs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_nanotube" title="Carbon nanotube">nanotubes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-buckyballs_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-buckyballs-30">[30]</a></sup> Their discoverers&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curl" title="Robert Curl">Robert Curl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Kroto" class="mw-redirect" title="Harold Kroto">Harold Kroto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Smalley" title="Richard Smalley">Richard Smalley</a>&#160;– received the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prize</a> in Chemistry in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> The resulting renewed interest in new forms lead to the discovery of further exotic allotropes, including <a href="/wiki/Glassy_carbon" title="Glassy carbon">glassy carbon</a>, and the realization that "<a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>" is not strictly <a href="/wiki/Amorphous" class="mw-redirect" title="Amorphous">amorphous</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-glassy_carbon_37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-glassy_carbon-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Graphite">Graphite</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a></div>
<p>Commercially viable natural deposits of graphite occur in many parts of the world, but the most important sources economically are in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. Graphite deposits are of <a href="/wiki/Metamorphic_rock" title="Metamorphic rock">metamorphic</a> origin, found in association with <a href="/wiki/Quartz" title="Quartz">quartz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mica" title="Mica">mica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feldspar" title="Feldspar">feldspars</a> in schists, <a href="/wiki/Gneiss" title="Gneiss">gneisses</a> and metamorphosed <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> as <a href="/wiki/Lens_(geology)" title="Lens (geology)">lenses</a> or <a href="/wiki/Vein_(geology)" title="Vein (geology)">veins</a>, sometimes of a metre or more in thickness. Deposits of graphite in <a href="/wiki/Borrowdale" title="Borrowdale">Borrowdale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cumberland" title="Cumberland">Cumberland</a>, <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> were at first of sufficient size and purity that, until the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Pencil" title="Pencil">pencils</a> were made simply by sawing blocks of natural graphite into strips before encasing the strips in wood. Today, smaller deposits of graphite are obtained by crushing the parent rock and floating the lighter graphite out on water.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are three types of natural graphite—amorphous, flake or crystalline flake, and vein or lump. Amorphous graphite is the lowest quality and most abundant. Contrary to science, in industry "amorphous" refers to very small crystal size rather than complete lack of crystal structure. Amorphous is used for lower value graphite products and is the lowest priced graphite. Large amorphous graphite deposits are found in China, Europe, Mexico and the United States. Flake graphite is less common and of higher quality than amorphous; it occurs as separate plates that crystallized in metamorphic rock. Flake graphite can be four times the price of amorphous. Good quality flakes can be processed into expandable graphite for many uses, such as <a href="/wiki/Flame_retardant" title="Flame retardant">flame retardants</a>. The foremost deposits are found in Austria, Brazil, Canada, China, Germany and Madagascar. Vein or lump graphite is the rarest, most valuable, and highest quality type of natural graphite. It occurs in veins along intrusive contacts in solid lumps, and it is only commercially mined in Sri Lanka.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/USGS" class="mw-redirect" title="USGS">USGS</a>, world production of natural graphite was 1.1&#160;million tonnes in 2010, to which China contributed 800,000&#160;t, India 130,000 t, Brazil 76,000&#160;t, North Korea 30,000&#160;t and Canada 25,000 t. No natural graphite was reported mined in the United States, but 118,000 t of synthetic graphite with an estimated value of $998&#160;million was produced in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-USGS_99-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diamond">Diamond</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">Diamond</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png/440px-Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png" width="440" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png/660px-Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png/880px-Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_Diamond_Output_in_2005.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Diamond output in 2005</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The diamond supply chain is controlled by a limited number of powerful businesses, and is also highly concentrated in a small number of locations around the world (see figure).</p>
<p>Only a very small fraction of the diamond ore consists of actual diamonds. The ore is crushed, during which care has to be taken in order to prevent larger diamonds from being destroyed in this process and subsequently the particles are sorted by density. Today, diamonds are located in the diamond-rich density fraction with the help of <a href="/wiki/X-ray_fluorescence" title="X-ray fluorescence">X-ray fluorescence</a>, after which the final sorting steps are done by hand. Before the use of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> became commonplace, the separation was done with grease belts; diamonds have a stronger tendency to stick to grease than the other minerals in the ore.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically diamonds were known to be found only in alluvial deposits in <a href="/wiki/Southern_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern India">southern India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Catelle1_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Catelle1-101">[101]</a></sup> India led the world in diamond production from the time of their discovery in approximately the 9th century BC<sup id="cite_ref-Ball_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ball-102">[102]</a></sup> to the mid-18th century AD, but the commercial potential of these sources had been exhausted by the late 18th century and at that time India was eclipsed by Brazil where the first non-Indian diamonds were found in 1725.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>Diamond production of primary deposits (kimberlites and lamproites) only started in the 1870s after the discovery of the Diamond fields in South Africa. Production has increased over time and now an accumulated total of 4.5&#160;billion carats have been mined since that date.<sup id="cite_ref-giasummer2007_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giasummer2007-104">[104]</a></sup> About 20% of that amount has been mined in the last 5 years alone, and during the last ten years 9 new mines have started production while 4 more are waiting to be opened soon. Most of these mines are located in Canada, Zimbabwe, Angola, and one in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-giasummer2007_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giasummer2007-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States, diamonds have been found in <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DGemGLorenz_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DGemGLorenz-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In 2004, a startling discovery of a microscopic diamond in the United States<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> led to the January 2008 bulk-sampling of <a href="/wiki/Kimberlite_pipes" class="mw-redirect" title="Kimberlite pipes">kimberlite pipes</a> in a remote part of <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today, most commercially viable diamond deposits are in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Congo" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of Congo">Democratic Republic of Congo</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> In 2005, Russia produced almost one-fifth of the global diamond output, reports the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a>. Australia has the richest diamantiferous pipe with production reaching peak levels of 42 metric tons (41 long tons; 46 short tons) per year in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-DGemGLorenz_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DGemGLorenz-105">[105]</a></sup> There are also commercial deposits being actively mined in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a> of <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> (mostly in <a href="/wiki/Sakha_Republic" title="Sakha Republic">Yakutia territory</a>; for example, <a href="/wiki/Mir_Mine" class="mw-redirect" title="Mir Mine">Mir pipe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Udachnaya_pipe" title="Udachnaya pipe">Udachnaya pipe</a>), Brazil, and in Northern and Western <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.</p>

</div>
</div>
<p>Carbon is essential to all known living systems, and without it life as we know it could not exist (see <a href="/wiki/Alternative_biochemistry" class="mw-redirect" title="Alternative biochemistry">alternative biochemistry</a>). The major economic use of carbon other than food and wood is in the form of hydrocarbons, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> gas and <a href="/wiki/Crude_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Crude oil">crude oil</a> (petroleum). <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">Crude oil</a> is <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilled</a> in <a href="/wiki/Refinery" title="Refinery">refineries</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical_industry" title="Petrochemical industry">petrochemical industry</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, and other products. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulose</a> is a natural, carbon-containing polymer produced by plants in the form of <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">Cellulose</a> is used primarily for maintaining structure in plants. Commercially valuable carbon polymers of animal origin include <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cashmere_wool" title="Cashmere wool">cashmere</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plastics" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastics">Plastics</a> are made from synthetic carbon polymers, often with oxygen and nitrogen atoms included at regular intervals in the main polymer chain. The raw materials for many of these synthetic substances come from crude oil.</p>
<p>The uses of carbon and its compounds are extremely varied. It can form <a href="/wiki/Alloys" class="mw-redirect" title="Alloys">alloys</a> with <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, of which the most common is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_steel" title="Carbon steel">carbon steel</a>. <a href="/wiki/Graphite" title="Graphite">Graphite</a> is combined with <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clays</a> to form the 'lead' used in <a href="/wiki/Pencil" title="Pencil">pencils</a> used for <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drawing" title="Drawing">drawing</a>. It is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Lubricant" title="Lubricant">lubricant</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a>, as a molding material in <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> manufacture, in <a href="/wiki/Electrodes" class="mw-redirect" title="Electrodes">electrodes</a> for dry <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electroforming" title="Electroforming">electroforming</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Brush_(electric)" title="Brush (electric)">brushes</a> for <a href="/wiki/Electric_motors" class="mw-redirect" title="Electric motors">electric motors</a> and as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactors" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear reactors">nuclear reactors</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">Charcoal</a> is used as a drawing material in <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">artwork</a>, barbecue <a href="/wiki/Grilling" title="Grilling">grilling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron_smelting" class="mw-redirect" title="Iron smelting">iron smelting</a>, and in many other applications. Wood, coal and oil are used as <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a> for production of energy and <a href="/wiki/Heating" class="mw-redirect" title="Heating">heating</a>. Gem quality <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> is used in jewelry, and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_diamond" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial diamond">industrial diamonds</a> are used in drilling, cutting and polishing tools for machining metals and stone. Plastics are made from fossil hydrocarbons, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon fiber">carbon fiber</a>, made by <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">pyrolysis</a> of synthetic <a href="/wiki/Polyester" title="Polyester">polyester</a> <a href="/wiki/Fiber" title="Fiber">fibers</a> is used to reinforce plastics to form advanced, lightweight <a href="/wiki/Composite_materials" class="mw-redirect" title="Composite materials">composite materials</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon fiber">Carbon fiber</a> is made by pyrolysis of extruded and stretched filaments of <a href="/wiki/Polyacrylonitrile" title="Polyacrylonitrile">polyacrylonitrile</a> (PAN) and other organic substances. The crystallographic structure and mechanical properties of the fiber depend on the type of starting material, and on the subsequent processing. Carbon fibers made from PAN have structure resembling narrow filaments of graphite, but thermal processing may re-order the structure into a continuous rolled sheet. The result is fibers with higher <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">specific tensile strength</a> than steel.<sup id="cite_ref-cantwell_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cantwell-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">Carbon black</a> is used as the black <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> in <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> <a href="/wiki/Ink" title="Ink">ink</a>, artist's oil paint and water colours, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_paper" title="Carbon paper">carbon paper</a>, automotive finishes, <a href="/wiki/India_ink" title="India ink">India ink</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laser_printer" class="mw-redirect" title="Laser printer">laser printer</a> <a href="/wiki/Toner" title="Toner">toner</a>. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">Carbon black</a> is also used as a <a href="/wiki/Filler_(materials)" title="Filler (materials)">filler</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rubber" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubber">rubber</a> products such as tyres and in <a href="/wiki/Plastic" title="Plastic">plastic</a> compounds. <a href="/wiki/Activated_charcoal" class="mw-redirect" title="Activated charcoal">Activated charcoal</a> is used as an <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(chemistry)" title="Absorption (chemistry)">absorbent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adsorbent" class="mw-redirect" title="Adsorbent">adsorbent</a> in <a href="/wiki/Filter_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Filter (chemistry)">filter</a> material in applications as diverse as <a href="/wiki/Gas_masks" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas masks">gas masks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_purification" title="Water purification">water purification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kitchen" title="Kitchen">kitchen</a> <a href="/wiki/Extractor_hood" class="mw-redirect" title="Extractor hood">extractor hoods</a>, and in medicine to <a href="/wiki/Absorption_(chemistry)" title="Absorption (chemistry)">absorb</a> toxins, poisons, or gases from the <a href="/wiki/Human_digestive_system" title="Human digestive system">digestive system</a>. Carbon is used in <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">chemical reduction</a> at high temperatures. <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">Coke</a> is used to reduce iron ore into iron (smelting). <a href="/wiki/Case_hardening" class="mw-redirect" title="Case hardening">Case hardening</a> of steel is achieved by heating finished steel components in carbon powder. <a href="/wiki/Carbide" title="Carbide">Carbides</a> of <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tungsten_carbide" title="Tungsten carbide">tungsten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boron_carbide" title="Boron carbide">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titanium_carbide" title="Titanium carbide">titanium</a>, are among the hardest known materials, and are used as <a href="/wiki/Abrasives" class="mw-redirect" title="Abrasives">abrasives</a> in cutting and grinding tools. Carbon compounds make up most of the materials used in clothing, such as natural and synthetic <a href="/wiki/Textiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Textiles">textiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leather" title="Leather">leather</a>, and almost all of the interior surfaces in the <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a> other than glass, stone and metal.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diamonds">Diamonds</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamond</a> industry falls into two categories: one dealing with gem-grade diamonds and the other, with industrial-grade diamonds. While a large trade in both types of diamonds exists, the two markets act in dramatically different ways.</p>
<p>Unlike <a href="/wiki/Precious_metal" title="Precious metal">precious metals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> or <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a>, gem diamonds do not trade as a <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a>: there is a substantial mark-up in the sale of diamonds, and there is not a very active market for resale of diamonds.</p>
<p>Industrial diamonds are valued mostly for their hardness and heat conductivity, with the gemological qualities of clarity and color being mostly irrelevant. About 80% of mined diamonds (equal to about 100 million carats or 20&#160;tonnes annually) are unsuitable for use as gemstones are relegated for industrial use (known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bort" title="Bort">bort</a>)</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_diamond" title="Synthetic diamond">synthetic diamonds</a>, invented in the 1950s, found almost immediate industrial applications; 3&#160;billion carats (600&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>) of synthetic diamond is produced annually.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The dominant industrial use of diamond is in cutting, drilling, grinding, and polishing. Most of these applications do not require large diamonds; in fact, most diamonds of gem-quality except for their small size can be used industrially. Diamonds are embedded in drill tips or saw blades, or ground into a powder for use in grinding and polishing applications.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Specialized applications include use in laboratories as containment for <a href="/wiki/Pressure_experiment" title="Pressure experiment">high pressure experiments</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Diamond_anvil_cell" title="Diamond anvil cell">diamond anvil cell</a>), high-performance <a href="/wiki/Bearing_(mechanical)" title="Bearing (mechanical)">bearings</a>, and limited use in specialized <a href="/wiki/Window" title="Window">windows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> With the continuing advances in the production of synthetic diamonds, new applications are becoming feasible. Garnering much excitement is the possible use of diamond as a <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> suitable for <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">microchips</a>, and because of its exceptional heat conductance property, as a <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a> in <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precautions">Precautions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg/170px-Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg/255px-Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg/340px-Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4544" data-file-height="6043" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Worker_at_carbon_black_plant2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Worker at <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">carbon black</a> plant in <a href="/wiki/Sunray,_Texas" title="Sunray, Texas">Sunray, Texas</a> (photo by <a href="/wiki/John_Vachon" title="John Vachon">John Vachon</a>, 1942)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pure carbon has extremely low <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> to humans and can be handled and even ingested safely in the form of graphite or charcoal. It is resistant to dissolution or chemical attack, even in the acidic contents of the digestive tract. Consequently, once it enters into the body's tissues it is likely to remain there indefinitely. <a href="/wiki/Carbon_black" title="Carbon black">Carbon black</a> was probably one of the first pigments to be used for <a href="/wiki/Tattoo" title="Tattoo">tattooing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" class="mw-redirect" title="Ötzi the Iceman">Ötzi the Iceman</a> was found to have carbon tattoos that survived during his life and for 5200&#160;years after his death.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Inhalation of coal dust or soot (carbon black) in large quantities can be dangerous, irritating lung tissues and causing the congestive <a href="/wiki/Human_lung" class="mw-redirect" title="Human lung">lung</a> disease, <a href="/wiki/Coalworker%27s_pneumoconiosis" title="Coalworker's pneumoconiosis">coalworker's pneumoconiosis</a>. Diamond dust used as an abrasive can harmful if ingested or inhaled. Microparticles of carbon are produced in diesel engine exhaust fumes, and may accumulate in the lungs.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> In these examples, the harm may result from contaminants (e.g., organic chemicals, heavy metals) rather than from the carbon itself.</p>
<p>Carbon generally has low toxicity to <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>; but carbon nanoparticles are deadly to <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila" title="Drosophila">Drosophila</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon may burn vigorously and brightly in the presence of air at high temperatures. Large accumulations of coal, which have remained inert for hundreds of millions of years in the absence of oxygen, may <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_combustion" title="Spontaneous combustion">spontaneously combust</a> when exposed to air in coal mine waste tips, ship cargo holds and coal bunkers,<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> and storage dumps.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear applications</a> where graphite is used as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a>, accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Wigner_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Wigner energy">Wigner energy</a> followed by a sudden, spontaneous release may occur. <a href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">Annealing</a> to at least 250&#160;°C can release the energy safely, although in the <a href="/wiki/Windscale_fire" title="Windscale fire">Windscale fire</a> the procedure went wrong, causing other reactor materials to combust.</p>
<p>The great variety of carbon compounds include such lethal poisons as <a href="/wiki/Tetrodotoxin" title="Tetrodotoxin">tetrodotoxin</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Lectin" title="Lectin">lectin</a> <a href="/wiki/Ricin" title="Ricin">ricin</a> from seeds of the <a href="/wiki/Castor_oil_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Castor oil plant">castor oil plant</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Ricinus_communis" class="mw-redirect" title="Ricinus communis">Ricinus communis</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Cyanide" title="Cyanide">cyanide</a> (CN<sup>−</sup>), and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide</a>; and such essentials to life as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>.</p>
